# Text Elements
Ray Tracing ^aSO9XnFy

So far we have modeled objects in a scene with light sources
this is yet to be a realistic simulation because we are missing
key information like shadows, transparency and mirrors ^Y2ul8lcl

Whitted Recursive ray tracing ^ecKwdkk9

In order to simulate shadows and reflection/refraction we need to trace the rays
that are projected from the light-source and how they end up reaching the eye ^MbV76pGT

we assume in this case that all light sources are point light sources
we also assume all materials already have diffuse and specular terms
Only opaque objects create shadows
reflection and refraction are restricted to ideal ^Q1bBIIxS

a naive approach ^8mxwHvRh

We trace all the points coming from the light
source and where they end up on the eye ^8MVuVzVg

this is bad and very expensive because there is
an infinite amount of rays coming from it and
we dont know how many bounces it needs for one ray
to reach the eye (even if we only take ideal reflection) ^4Lia0CEm

How about we trace all rays shot from the eye and
into the light source? ^lTeHOaRs

since this reflection relation is symmetric this is possible,
we set the eye at camera position and divide our projection
plane into pixels with desired resolution, we shoot a ray per pixel
untill it intersects an object and then we compute the following:
1) its color using diffuse and specular term
2) whether this point is shadowed by another intersection on its way to the light source
3) whether this point is reflective (and if so, reflect the ideal reflection intersection)
4) whether this point is refractive (and if so refract it depending on parameters) ^V6G8LYFT

Still very slow ^IEbfrAsR

This raytracing's method is quite complex
best case scenario it ends up with O(mn) complexity
such that n is the number of primitives in the scene
and m is the number of primary rays shot ^kWzEtxAf

and this increases with the amount of bounces,
amount of light sources and many reflective
objects ^rx8ltKwn

Ways to optimize raytracing ^dXC5VxpH

Fewer rays? ^Wwmd3bUM

Faster/Fewer intersections? ^5QMPwedv

Adaptive Recursion depth

Perhaps it is wise to limit the maximal depth of recursion that
we can call, that way after x bounces, the rays disappear!
we model this by allowing the reflected and refracted rays to contribute
only to some decreasing fraction of the color, and if this fraction gets too small
we stop the ray! ^t3tubrKI

Bounding Volumes ^g1FWl0cR

in order to speed up the process of intersect testing each pixel's ray
with every primitive on the scene, we form hierarchical bounding volumes
that contain local ecosystems of objects.
These objects need to be simple enough to allow us to speed up the
intersection tests but also efficient enough to truly contain as little
empty space as possible ^1ux7ALTq

Spatial Subdivision ^TUr4vAjz

Alongside the bounding volumes we can also add for each mesh some sort of
spatial datastructure to quickly find out which triangles we need to perform tests on.
some of which are kd-trees, grids, octrees, quadtrees ^aUcykPlf

our triangles being spread around in our grid, we can quickly identify using
Bresenhams algorithm in 3d, which grids are being intersected and thus
we can find out which group of triangles to perform the test on while ignoring
the others ^GYdOMXg5

We only really need to know the first intersection! ^VHmGptIF

Light buffer ^jSvldmUv

The idea revolves around precomputed some useful information about our
light source in order to reduce the intersection computations.

We create a grid around our light source and shoot out rays from
the center of each pixel and we store the distance of the minimum intersection
that occurs from that pixel.

This allows us in real time to check whether the ray shot from the eye onto a primitive is the
closest intersection that the light buffer has and if not then its shadowed ^sReylnbI

Small issue regarding aliasing
of shadows ^a9o6cEmw

this pixel corrosponds to multiple
primitives within our mesh and some of which
will be further away then others leaving 
some of them shadowed by primtives that are
next to them! ^OmI84pDv

We can store the ID of the polygons that way I can have an ID test to check
whether the intersections belong to the same polygon! ^qI0l3TBU

Not very expensive to store, its
practically in the rasterization hardware
like the z buffer ^66Mcs5ed

We can also simulate
other effects using this ^SNKGvjTF

Soft Shadows ^eOIXunOK

Shadow rays are distributed over area light source
Soft shadow results from some rays being occluded or unoccluded 
Distribution of rays on light source can be precomputed Uniformly 
Regular grids have to be jittered to avoid aliasing ^Y7f9bgTU

send many rays ^pyDL6SZI

Gloss ^8fm9ajWJ

We can reflect the light off of objects
based on their material parameters and allow
for glossyness effects like in phong model ^dpg1DCS0

Same idea with translucency ^qQDw6cOY

Motion Blur ^3JB1B8ib

Motion Blur is the exact same ray tracing
algorithm performed on different time measurments
allowing for the light computations to "lag behind" ^m9OV51M1

In a similar fashion raytracing can also
simulate depth field such that theres
a focal point in the scene and anything closer
or further away is not in focus

this is done via shooting multiple jittered rays and
running them through the focal point ^oc2ji3Hw

PROS ^P89AIORk

CONS ^aTMDIBBm

V.S ^3GLRciyM

Not a very complex algorithm, only requires ray
and intersection computation ^WJmdLQje

includes occlusion, shadowing, reflection, refraction,
perspective transformation, clipping automatically ^xvOo27gQ

shadowing is only computed in relevant points ^K0MoZUq5

objects may intersect each other ^flk8dC7m

Sampling creates a lot of aliasing
which is expensive to get rid of ^0qTvtWNo

computational effort is high ^gn4ulpQx

soft shadows are really expensive ^SKlbcBmu

no coherency exploited and the computation
needs to be redone everytime the view changes ^thoPgwAd

No diffuse light exchange ^6XV2weLq

even non-mirror like surfaces illuminate our scene by reflecting parts of rays
which is ignored if theyre diffuse materials! ^VSIdpqeH

Rendering ^LRHY3nCY

Lets model real light transport equations and try our best to make it computationally plausible
There are many forms to this equation but this is the three point form ^wRdjhH9g

Our goal and what
we want to know
Radiance
How much light from
point x is being sent to
x'  ^iOruQmxC

Emitted radiance
from x to x'
this is a value
that is given
when x is a light
source ^kwaU9M2f

sum over the
contribution of
all other surfaces
in the scene ^MLv3XQzz

Radiance sent
from x'' towards
our point x
from every x'' surface
in the scene ^RXnL0DuI

The scale that it
is being 
reflected by
which is a 
property for
the material ^5mmMnRoV

Visibility term since
we dont want
surfaces with no
LOS to contribute
1 if not occluded
0 if occluded ^iORb0Da4

The part of the radiance
of x'' that is going
towards x due to
the geometric shape
of the surface
of x''
(Per angle) ^whs6qEMM

Geometric relations ^LyFMQyjK

Geometric relations between the sender and reciever of a ray
matter much to how much of that ray contribution is actually
getting there, orientation angles and distances matter! ^1SrT2xTC

Orientation                    Angles                   Distance ^o4HUR56M

Radiosity ^KO2twBJV

How do we solve
the integral equation? ^nfKjO4Tq

Lets make a few assumptions to simplify our equation from its current state ^2EHqoMBu

1) All surfaces are perfectly diffuse i.e reflection equal in all directions

2) scene is divided into small elements such that Bi is the total radiosity leaving element i

3) Radiosity on each surface is constant ^usikFFX5

Independent of view
direction ^iYzFy4yh

x ^cDBQb2VP

x' ^0mK1ciLD

x'' ^aOe1gNBh

no longer
depends on
direction ^1eQ4xQTA

is now just a number
and doesnt rely
on x''s ray sent to
x and to x' ^ZeOq5iWA

Form factor F
tells us how much light arrives
at x from x''
0 if occluded ^tnJ42Y0G

Discretize into smaller
finite elements ^w4dzano6

Linear system
of equations ^jiGOtNpt

THis is a bit too
large for computations
depending on the scene ^tlLeoCLb

We can perform an iterative solution such that
we update the value of Bi by getting the product of row i
with the existing radiosities vector ^2AOXCHtR

Which is like updating on element of the scene according to
all the elements that illuminate it ^wvmmm4JJ

Then once we have the
center value we can
interpolate it using
its neighbours to find out
the vertex value
and perform our normal shading ^zJ8xbfFm

Since it is constant ^KFGLfDgh

Half sphere
light distribution ^caYEqku2

Scale down by
Receivers volume ^kGjEqA88

How do we efficiently
compute this integral
over the small sized elements? ^tkMz8kjE

Lets take in this analogy ^YOsPpBst

emitting ^wg0bSH5o

recieving ^HLQYPa1V

projected shape is
equivalent to the form
factor ^umx73r9R

unit hemisphere ^vQdGSrLO

unit disk ^itg1Juzv

Hemicube algorithm ^A6DIihQH

we construct a hemicube around each surface element
we divide this hemicubes faces into pixels and we project
the light onto this pixels, this gives us a value for each pixel
the final form factor is the sum of those pixels
occlusion is already accounted for in zbuffer ^vUIdXFyJ

Its made as a cube to utilize parallelization of gpus ^RynYWxKA

Ray Casting ^92rwcX9E

Generate n rays and shoot them from one element
to the other (subsampling) and integrate the
the forms between them ^84O9f7hH

One thing to point out is that
form factor calculation takes up
the majority of the computation time thus
it is very expensive and we look for
alternatives ^mld5jzLs

Progressive Refinement ^ujoJ5Qjh

Instead of gathering radiosity from every contributor
we now shoot rays from one element to all the other ones

Whys this faster?

You can always start from the brightest patches and work
your way down, such that at any point you decide to stop, you
have already computed the major contributors to the scene's lighting ^QsrQGK2L

Hierarchical Radiosity ^ooGUpqR5

Start with large patches and have a way
to figure out if the solution for the large patch
is accurate enough and only if it isnt we go down
to smaller patches ^MtP2PIbQ

Change of basis ^dU1TbmVI

Smart math thing ^s3evUgfN

To conclude:
1) Accurately physics approach representation of diffuse scenes
2) correctly handles colour bleeding and soft shadows and indirect lighting
3) Independent of view
4) Pre-computed scenes
5) illumination of scene are based on physical parameters
6) Restricted to diffuse
7) Expensive ^dpLetaPs

Monte Carlo Ray-tracing (Path-Tracing) ^1RqxxEVZ

Main idea: Subsampling of the integral! ^zcQSgYre

This equation no longer points to a point x''
instead it calculates a value using an angle on the unit halfsphere ^U0wwHocK

Theta_i indicates the incoming radiosity's angle and wi is the angle we
integrate over to send rays in a halfsphere
we no longer care about distances, only angles!
Visbility is implicit in the integration domain ^SGZJ8oBg

Scattering ^TDJnJWe6

Reflecting ^rPDlkV5d

Solving this integral of infinite dimensions is impossible.
thus we simplify and subsample in order to approximate it ^YHn017Mc

We chop the integral into a finite amount
of rectangles and perform the area under
the curve approximation by summing them up ^lk4Im0W9

thus we perform
random subsampling and the more samples we pick
the more accurate our result is ^GUfHyk3W

considering multiple bounces this computation complexity scales quadratically!!
i.e for every surface we consider 2 dimensions of bouncing, and for each bounce
we consider more dimensions of bouncing which keeps going with the number of
bounces ^TVW8Z8xH

This converges with ^3gV2OP51

and in high dimensions produces accurate results if we increase N
(low values of N produce a lot of variance) ^VpXmh7pN

What is our sample xi? ^UFvKuQy4

A sample is a collection of randomly sampled
angles for each bounce that the ray encounters
Which defines a path through the scene ^JuX12UnW

Increasing Efficiency ^1hyOS1Y0

1) We want to bias our sampler such that it mostly picks rays from areas
we know will contribute the most towards the colour of our scene (light)
Careful not to make your sampling too biased!

2) the chances of sampling random angles and reaching the light is unlikely thus
alongside the sampleing we always send rays in the direction of the light to
compute the lighting

3) Denoise the image to reduce the variance produces by subsampling ^Kem8ZAvR

In conclusion ^dGY9GPHo

1) We dont simplify our rendering model too much

2) easily parallelized for each sample

3) can be used for any geometry that can be intersected by a ray

4) no discretization of the scene elements

5) simple implementationm

6) easily improved by drawing more samples ^haQyDnII
